<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:John_Locke%27s_Kit-cat_portrait_by_Godfrey_Kneller,_National_Portrait_Gallery,_London.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3088" data-file-width="2056" decoding="async" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/John_Locke%27s_Kit-cat_portrait_by_Godfrey_Kneller%2C_National_Portrait_Gallery%2C_London.JPG/220px-John_Locke%27s_Kit-cat_portrait_by_Godfrey_Kneller%2C_National_Portrait_Gallery%2C_London.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/John_Locke%27s_Kit-cat_portrait_by_Godfrey_Kneller%2C_National_Portrait_Gallery%2C_London.JPG/330px-John_Locke%27s_Kit-cat_portrait_by_Godfrey_Kneller%2C_National_Portrait_Gallery%2C_London.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/John_Locke%27s_Kit-cat_portrait_by_Godfrey_Kneller%2C_National_Portrait_Gallery%2C_London.JPG/440px-John_Locke%27s_Kit-cat_portrait_by_Godfrey_Kneller%2C_National_Portrait_Gallery%2C_London.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:John_Locke%27s_Kit-cat_portrait_by_Godfrey_Kneller,_National_Portrait_Gallery,_London.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>John Locke's portrait by <a href="/wiki/Godfrey_Kneller" title="Godfrey Kneller">Godfrey Kneller</a>, National Portrait Gallery, London</div></div></div>
<p><b>John Locke</b> <span class="noexcerpt nowraplinks" style="font-size:85%; font-weight:normal;"><a href="/wiki/Fellow_of_the_Royal_Society" title="Fellow of the Royal Society">FRS</a></span> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/ɒ/: 'o' in 'body'">ɒ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span></span>/</a></span></span>; 29 August 1632 – 28 October 1704) was an English philosopher and physician, widely regarded as one of the most influential of <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Enlightenment</a> thinkers and commonly known as the "Father of <a href="/wiki/Liberalism" title="Liberalism">Liberalism</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Considered one of the first of the British <a href="/wiki/Empiricism" title="Empiricism">empiricists</a>, following the tradition of Sir <a href="/wiki/Francis_Bacon" title="Francis Bacon">Francis Bacon</a>, Locke is equally important to <a href="/wiki/Social_contract" title="Social contract">social contract</a> theory. His work greatly affected the development of <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">epistemology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Political_philosophy" title="Political philosophy">political philosophy</a>. His writings influenced <a href="/wiki/Voltaire" title="Voltaire">Voltaire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jean-Jacques_Rousseau" title="Jean-Jacques Rousseau">Jean-Jacques Rousseau</a>, and many <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Enlightenment" title="Scottish Enlightenment">Scottish Enlightenment</a> thinkers, as well as the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American Revolutionaries</a>. His contributions to <a href="/wiki/Classical_republicanism" title="Classical republicanism">classical republicanism</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liberal_theory" title="Liberal theory">liberal theory</a> are reflected in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence" title="United States Declaration of Independence">United States Declaration of Independence</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Internationally, Locke’s political-legal principles continue to have a profound influence on the theory and practice of limited representative government and the protection of basic rights and freedoms under the rule of law.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>Locke's <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_mind" title="Philosophy of mind">theory of mind</a> is often cited as the origin of modern conceptions of <a href="/wiki/Personal_identity" title="Personal identity"><i>identity</i></a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Self_(psychology)" title="Self (psychology)"><i>self</i></a>, figuring prominently in the work of later philosophers such as <a href="/wiki/Jean-Jacques_Rousseau" title="Jean-Jacques Rousseau">Jean-Jacques Rousseau</a>, <a href="/wiki/David_Hume" title="David Hume">David Hume</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Immanuel_Kant" title="Immanuel Kant">Immanuel Kant</a>. Locke was the first to define the <i>self</i> through a continuity of <a href="/wiki/Consciousness" title="Consciousness">consciousness</a>. He postulated that, at birth, the <a href="/wiki/Mind" title="Mind">mind</a> was a blank slate, or <i><a href="/wiki/Tabula_rasa" title="Tabula rasa">tabula rasa</a></i>. Contrary to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Descartes" title="Descartes">Cartesian</a> philosophy based on pre-existing concepts, he maintained that we are born without <a href="/wiki/Innatism" title="Innatism">innate ideas</a>, and that <a href="/wiki/Knowledge" title="Knowledge">knowledge</a> is instead determined only by experience derived from <a href="/wiki/Sense" title="Sense">sense</a> <a href="/wiki/Perception" title="Perception">perception</a>, a concept now known as <i><a href="/wiki/Empiricism" title="Empiricism">empiricism</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Demonstrating the ideology of science in his observations, whereby something must be capable of being tested repeatedly and that nothing is exempt from being disproved, Locke stated that "whatever I write, as soon as I discover it not to be true, my hand shall be the forwardest to throw it into the fire". Such is one example of Locke's belief in empiricism.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span></h2>
<p>Locke was born on 29 August 1632, in a small thatched <a href="/wiki/Cottage" title="Cottage">cottage</a> by the church in <a href="/wiki/Wrington" title="Wrington">Wrington</a>, Somerset, about 12 miles from <a href="/wiki/Bristol" title="Bristol">Bristol</a>. He was <a href="/wiki/Baptism" title="Baptism">baptised</a> the same day, as both of his parents were <a href="/wiki/Puritans" title="Puritans">Puritans</a>. Locke's father, also called John, was an attorney who served as clerk to the <a href="/wiki/Justice_of_the_peace" title="Justice of the peace">Justices of the Peace</a> in <a href="/wiki/Chew_Magna" title="Chew Magna">Chew Magna</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> and as a captain of <a href="/wiki/Cavalry" title="Cavalry">cavalry</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Roundhead" title="Roundhead">Parliamentarian</a> forces during the early part of the <a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War" title="English Civil War">English Civil War</a>. His mother was Agnes Keene. Soon after Locke's birth, the family moved to the <a href="/wiki/Market_town" title="Market town">market town</a> of <a href="/wiki/Pensford" title="Pensford">Pensford</a>, about seven miles south of Bristol, where Locke grew up in a rural <a href="/wiki/Tudor_architecture" title="Tudor architecture">Tudor</a> house in <a href="/wiki/Belluton" title="Belluton">Belluton</a>.
</p><p>In 1647, Locke was sent to the prestigious <a href="/wiki/Westminster_School" title="Westminster School">Westminster School</a> in London under the sponsorship of <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Popham" title="Alexander Popham">Alexander Popham</a>, a member of Parliament and John Sr.'s former commander. After completing studies there, he was admitted to <a href="/wiki/Christ_Church,_Oxford" title="Christ Church, Oxford">Christ Church</a>, <a href="/wiki/University_of_Oxford" title="University of Oxford">Oxford</a>, in the autumn of 1652 at the age of 20. The dean of the college at the time was <a href="/wiki/John_Owen_(theologian)" title="John Owen (theologian)">John Owen</a>, vice-chancellor of the university. Although a capable student, Locke was irritated by the undergraduate curriculum of the time. He found the works of modern philosophers, such as <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="René Descartes">René Descartes</a>, more interesting than the <a href="/wiki/Classics" title="Classics">classical</a> material taught at the university. Through his friend <a href="/wiki/Richard_Lower_(physician)" title="Richard Lower (physician)">Richard Lower</a>, whom he knew from the Westminster School, Locke was introduced to medicine and the <a href="/wiki/Experimental_philosophy" title="Experimental philosophy">experimental philosophy</a> being pursued at other universities and in the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society</a>, of which he eventually became a member.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Locke was awarded a <a href="/wiki/Bachelor%27s_degree" title="Bachelor's degree">bachelor's degree</a> in February 1656 and a <a href="/wiki/Master%27s_degree" title="Master's degree">master's degree</a> in June 1658.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sep_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-sep-7">[7]</a></sup> He obtained a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bachelor_of_medicine" title="Bachelor of medicine">bachelor of medicine</a> in February 1675,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> having studied the subject extensively during his time at Oxford and, in addition to Lower, worked with such noted scientists and thinkers as <a href="/wiki/Robert_Boyle" title="Robert Boyle">Robert Boyle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Willis" title="Thomas Willis">Thomas Willis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Hooke" title="Robert Hooke">Robert Hooke</a>. In 1666, he met <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Ashley_Cooper,_1st_Earl_of_Shaftesbury" title="Anthony Ashley Cooper, 1st Earl of Shaftesbury">Anthony Ashley Cooper, Lord Ashley</a>, who had come to Oxford seeking treatment for a <a href="/wiki/Liver" title="Liver">liver</a> infection. Ashley was impressed with Locke and persuaded him to become part of his retinue.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Career">Career</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Work">Work</span></h3>
<p>Locke had been looking for a career and in 1667 moved into Ashley's home at Exeter House in London, to serve as his personal physician. In London, Locke resumed his medical studies under the tutelage of <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Sydenham" title="Thomas Sydenham">Thomas Sydenham</a>. Sydenham had a major effect on Locke's natural philosophical thinking – an effect that would become evident in <i><a href="/wiki/An_Essay_Concerning_Human_Understanding" title="An Essay Concerning Human Understanding">An Essay Concerning Human Understanding</a>.</i>
</p><p>Locke's medical knowledge was put to the test when Ashley's liver infection became life-threatening. Locke coordinated the advice of several physicians and was probably instrumental in persuading Ashley to undergo surgery (then life-threatening in itself) to remove the cyst. Ashley survived and prospered, crediting Locke with saving his life.
</p><p>During this time, Locke served as Secretary of the <a href="/wiki/Board_of_Trade" title="Board of Trade">Board of Trade</a> and Plantations and Secretary to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lords_Proprietor" title="Lords Proprietor">Lords Proprietors</a> of <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Carolina" title="Province of Carolina">Carolina</a>, which helped to shape his ideas on international trade and economics.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Ashley, as a founder of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_Whig_Party" title="British Whig Party">Whig</a> movement, exerted great influence on Locke's political ideas. Locke became involved in politics when Ashley became <a href="/wiki/Lord_Chancellor" title="Lord Chancellor">Lord Chancellor</a> in 1672 (Ashley being created <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Shaftesbury" title="Earl of Shaftesbury">1st Earl of Shaftesbury</a> in 1673). Following Shaftesbury's fall from favour in 1675, Locke spent some time travelling across France as a tutor and medical attendant to <a href="/wiki/Caleb_Banks" title="Caleb Banks">Caleb Banks</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Henning_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Henning-22">[22]</a></sup> He returned to England in 1679 when Shaftesbury's political fortunes took a brief positive turn. Around this time, most likely at Shaftesbury's prompting, Locke composed the bulk of the <i><a href="/wiki/Two_Treatises_of_Government" title="Two Treatises of Government">Two Treatises of Government</a></i>. While it was once thought that Locke wrote the <i>Treatises</i> to defend the <a href="/wiki/Glorious_Revolution" title="Glorious Revolution">Glorious Revolution</a> of 1688, recent scholarship has shown that the work was composed well before this date.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELaslett1988III._''Two_Treatises_of_Government''_and_the_Revolution_of_1688_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELaslett1988III._''Two_Treatises_of_Government''_and_the_Revolution_of_1688-23">[23]</a></sup> The work is now viewed as a more general argument against <a href="/wiki/Absolute_monarchy" title="Absolute monarchy">absolute monarchy</a> (particularly as espoused by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Filmer" title="Robert Filmer">Robert Filmer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hobbes" title="Thomas Hobbes">Thomas Hobbes</a>) and for individual consent as the basis of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Political_legitimacy" title="Political legitimacy">political legitimacy</a>. Although Locke was associated with the influential Whigs, his ideas about <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_and_legal_rights" title="Natural and legal rights">natural rights</a> and government are today considered quite revolutionary for that period in English history.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Netherlands">The Netherlands</span></h3>
<p>Locke fled to the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a> in 1683, under strong suspicion of involvement in the <a href="/wiki/Rye_House_Plot" title="Rye House Plot">Rye House Plot</a>, although there is little evidence to suggest that he was directly involved in the scheme. The philosopher and novelist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rebecca_Newberger_Goldstein" title="Rebecca Newberger Goldstein">Rebecca Newberger Goldstein</a> argues that during his five years in Holland, Locke chose his friends "from among the same freethinking members of dissenting Protestant groups as <a href="/wiki/Baruch_Spinoza" title="Baruch Spinoza">Spinoza's</a> small group of loyal confidants. [Baruch Spinoza had died in 1677.] Locke almost certainly met men in Amsterdam who spoke of the ideas of that renegade Jew who... insisted on identifying himself through his religion of reason alone." While she says that "Locke's strong empiricist tendencies" would have "disinclined him to read a grandly metaphysical work such as Spinoza's <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethics_(Spinoza)" title="Ethics (Spinoza)">Ethics</a></i>, in other ways he was deeply receptive to Spinoza's ideas, most particularly to the rationalist's well thought out argument for political and <a href="/wiki/Religious_tolerance" title="Religious tolerance">religious tolerance</a> and the necessity of the separation of church and state."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> In the Netherlands, Locke had time to return to his writing, spending a great deal of time working on the <i>Essay Concerning Human Understanding</i> and composing the <i>Letter on Toleration.</i>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Return_to_England">Return to England</span></h3>
<p>Locke did not return home until after the <a href="/wiki/Glorious_Revolution" title="Glorious Revolution">Glorious Revolution</a>. Locke accompanied <a href="/wiki/Mary_II_of_England" title="Mary II of England">Mary II</a> back to England in 1688. The bulk of Locke's publishing took place upon his return from exile – his aforementioned <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Essay_Concerning_Human_Understanding" title="Essay Concerning Human Understanding">Essay Concerning Human Understanding</a></i>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Two_Treatises_of_Government" title="Two Treatises of Government">Two Treatises of Government</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/A_Letter_Concerning_Toleration" title="A Letter Concerning Toleration">A Letter Concerning Toleration</a></i> all appearing in quick succession.
</p><p>Locke's close friend <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lady_Masham" title="Lady Masham">Lady Masham</a> invited him to join her at Otes, the Mashams' country house in Essex. Although his time there was marked by variable health from <a href="/wiki/Asthma" title="Asthma">asthma</a> attacks, he nevertheless became an intellectual hero of the Whigs. During this period he discussed matters with such figures as <a href="/wiki/John_Dryden" title="John Dryden">John Dryden</a> and <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span></h3>
<p>He died on 28 October 1704, and is buried in the churchyard of the village of <a href="/wiki/High_Laver" title="High Laver">High Laver</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> east of <a href="/wiki/Harlow" title="Harlow">Harlow</a> in Essex, where he had lived in the household of Sir Francis Masham since 1691. Locke never married nor had children.
</p><p>Events that happened during Locke's lifetime include the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English_Restoration" title="English Restoration">English Restoration</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Great_Plague_of_London" title="Great Plague of London">Great Plague of London</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Great_Fire_of_London" title="Great Fire of London">Great Fire of London</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Glorious_Revolution" title="Glorious Revolution">Glorious Revolution</a>. He did not quite see the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Act_of_Union_1707" title="Act of Union 1707">Act of Union of 1707</a>, though the thrones of England and Scotland were held in <a href="/wiki/Personal_union" title="Personal union">personal union</a> throughout his lifetime. <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_monarchy" title="Constitutional monarchy">Constitutional monarchy</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parliamentary_democracy" title="Parliamentary democracy">parliamentary democracy</a> were in their infancy during Locke's time.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Philosophy">Philosophy</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:John_Locke_by_John_Greenhill.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2729" data-file-width="2245" decoding="async" height="267" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/John_Locke_by_John_Greenhill.jpg/220px-John_Locke_by_John_Greenhill.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/John_Locke_by_John_Greenhill.jpg/330px-John_Locke_by_John_Greenhill.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/John_Locke_by_John_Greenhill.jpg/440px-John_Locke_by_John_Greenhill.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:John_Locke_by_John_Greenhill.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait of John Locke by <a href="/wiki/John_Greenhill" title="John Greenhill">John Greenhill</a> (died 1676)</div></div></div>
<p>In the late 17th and early 18th centuries, Locke's <i><a href="/wiki/Two_Treatises_of_Government" title="Two Treatises of Government">Two Treatises</a></i> were rarely cited. Historian Julian Hoppit said of the book, "except among some Whigs, even as a contribution to the intense debate of the 1690s it made little impression and was generally ignored until 1703 (though in Oxford in 1695 it was reported to have made 'a great noise')."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Philipps_Kenyon" title="John Philipps Kenyon">John Kenyon</a>, in his study of British political debate from 1689 to 1720, has remarked that Locke's theories were "mentioned so rarely in the early stages of the [Glorious] Revolution, up to 1692, and even less thereafter, unless it was to heap abuse on them" and that "no one, including most Whigs, [was] ready for the idea of a notional or abstract contract of the kind adumbrated by Locke".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 200">: 200 </span></sup> In contrast, Kenyon adds that <a href="/wiki/Algernon_Sidney" title="Algernon Sidney">Algernon Sidney</a>'s <i>Discourses Concerning Government</i> were "certainly much more influential than Locke's <i>Two Treatises.</i>"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[i]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 51">: 51 </span></sup>
</p><p>In the 50 years after Queen Anne's death in 1714, the <i>Two Treatises</i> were reprinted only once (except in the collected works of Locke). However, with the rise of American resistance to British taxation, the <i><a href="/wiki/Two_Treatises_of_Government#Second_Treatise" title="Two Treatises of Government">Second Treatise of Government</a></i> gained a new readership; it was frequently cited in the debates in both America and Britain. The first American printing occurred in 1773 in Boston.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>
Locke exercised a profound influence on <a href="/wiki/Political_philosophy" title="Political philosophy">political philosophy</a>, in particular on modern liberalism. <a href="/wiki/Michael_Zuckert" title="Michael Zuckert">Michael Zuckert</a> has argued that Locke launched liberalism by tempering Hobbesian absolutism and clearly <a href="/wiki/Separation_of_church_and_state" title="Separation of church and state">separating the realms of Church and State</a>. He had a strong influence on <a href="/wiki/Voltaire" title="Voltaire">Voltaire</a>, who called him "<i>le sage</i> Locke". His arguments concerning <a href="/wiki/Liberty" title="Liberty">liberty</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Social_contract" title="Social contract">social contract</a> later influenced the written works of <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Hamilton" title="Alexander Hamilton">Alexander Hamilton</a>, <a href="/wiki/James_Madison" title="James Madison">James Madison</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a>, and other <a href="/wiki/Founding_Fathers_of_the_United_States" title="Founding Fathers of the United States">Founding Fathers of the United States</a>. In fact, one passage from the <i>Second Treatise</i> is reproduced verbatim in the Declaration of Independence, the reference to a "long train of abuses". Such was Locke's influence that Thomas Jefferson wrote:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[31]</a></sup></p><blockquote><p><a href="/wiki/Francis_Bacon" title="Francis Bacon">Bacon</a>, Locke and <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Newton</a>… I consider them as the three greatest men that have ever lived, without any exception, and as having laid the foundation of those superstructures which have been raised in the Physical and Moral sciences.</p></blockquote><p>However, Locke's influence may have been even more profound in the realm of <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">epistemology</a>. Locke redefined <a href="/wiki/Subjectivity" title="Subjectivity">subjectivity</a>, or <i>self</i>, leading <a href="/wiki/Intellectual_history" title="Intellectual history">intellectual historians</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Charles_Taylor_(philosopher)" title="Charles Taylor (philosopher)">Charles Taylor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jerrold_Seigel" title="Jerrold Seigel">Jerrold Seigel</a> to argue that Locke's <i><a href="/wiki/An_Essay_Concerning_Human_Understanding" title="An Essay Concerning Human Understanding">An Essay Concerning Human Understanding</a></i> (1689/90) marks the beginning of the modern Western conception of the <i>self</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>Locke's <a href="/wiki/Associationism" title="Associationism">theory of association</a> heavily influenced the subject matter of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Modern_psychology" title="Modern psychology">modern psychology</a>. At the time, Locke's recognition of two types of ideas, <i>simple</i> and <i>complex</i>—and, more importantly, their interaction through association—inspired other philosophers, such as <a href="/wiki/David_Hume" title="David Hume">David Hume</a> and <a href="/wiki/George_Berkeley" title="George Berkeley">George Berkeley</a>, to revise and expand this theory and apply it to explain how humans gain knowledge in the physical world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_tolerance">Religious tolerance</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Toleration#Locke" title="Toleration">Toleration § Locke</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:John_Locke_by_Richard_Westmacott.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3088" data-file-width="2056" decoding="async" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/John_Locke_by_Richard_Westmacott.jpg/220px-John_Locke_by_Richard_Westmacott.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/John_Locke_by_Richard_Westmacott.jpg/330px-John_Locke_by_Richard_Westmacott.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/John_Locke_by_Richard_Westmacott.jpg/440px-John_Locke_by_Richard_Westmacott.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:John_Locke_by_Richard_Westmacott.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>John Locke by <a href="/wiki/Richard_Westmacott" title="Richard Westmacott">Richard Westmacott</a>, University College London</div></div></div>
<p>Writing his <i><a href="/wiki/A_Letter_Concerning_Toleration" title="A Letter Concerning Toleration">Letters Concerning Toleration</a></i> (1689–1692) in the aftermath of the <a href="/wiki/European_wars_of_religion" title="European wars of religion">European wars of religion</a>, Locke formulated a classic reasoning for <a href="/wiki/Toleration" title="Toleration">religious tolerance</a>, in which three arguments are central:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li>earthly judges, <a href="/wiki/State_(polity)" title="State (polity)">the state</a> in particular, and human beings generally, cannot dependably evaluate the <a href="/wiki/Truth_claim" title="Truth claim">truth-claims</a> of competing religious standpoints;</li>
<li>even if they could, enforcing a single 'true religion' would not have the desired effect, because belief cannot be compelled by violence;</li>
<li>coercing <a href="/wiki/Religious_uniformity" title="Religious uniformity">religious uniformity</a> would lead to more social disorder than allowing diversity.</li></ol>
<p>With regard to his position on religious tolerance, Locke was influenced by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baptist" title="Baptist">Baptist</a> theologians like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Smyth_(Baptist_minister)" title="John Smyth (Baptist minister)">John Smyth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Helwys" title="Thomas Helwys">Thomas Helwys</a>, who had published <a href="/wiki/Tract_(literature)" title="Tract (literature)">tracts</a> demanding <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_thought" title="Freedom of thought">freedom of conscience</a> in the early 17th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeussi1956_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeussi1956-37">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOlmstead196018_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOlmstead196018-38">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[39]</a></sup> Baptist theologian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roger_Williams_(theologian)" title="Roger Williams (theologian)">Roger Williams</a> founded the colony of <a href="/wiki/Rhode_Island" title="Rhode Island">Rhode Island</a> in 1636, where he combined a <a href="/wiki/Democracy" title="Democracy">democratic constitution</a> with unlimited religious freedom. His tract, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Bloudy_Tenent_of_Persecution_for_Cause_of_Conscience" title="The Bloudy Tenent of Persecution for Cause of Conscience">The Bloudy Tenent of Persecution for Cause of Conscience</a></i> (1644), which was widely read in the mother country, was a passionate plea for absolute religious freedom and the total <a href="/wiki/Separation_of_church_and_state" title="Separation of church and state">separation of church and state</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOlmstead1960102–05_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOlmstead1960102–05-41">[40]</a></sup> Freedom of conscience had had high priority on the theological, philosophical, and political agenda, as <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther" title="Martin Luther">Martin Luther</a> refused to recant his beliefs before the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Imperial_Diet_participants_(1792)" title="List of Imperial Diet participants (1792)">Diet</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a> at <a href="/wiki/Diet_of_Worms" title="Diet of Worms">Worms</a> in 1521, unless he would be proved false by the Bible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOlmstead19605_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOlmstead19605-42">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Slavery_and_child_labour">Slavery and child labour</span></h3>
<p>Locke's views on slavery were multifaceted and complex. Although he wrote against slavery in general, Locke was an investor and beneficiary of the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_slave_trade" title="Atlantic slave trade">slave trading</a> <a href="/wiki/Royal_African_Company" title="Royal African Company">Royal Africa Company</a>. In addition, while secretary to the <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Ashley_Cooper,_1st_Earl_of_Shaftesbury" title="Anthony Ashley Cooper, 1st Earl of Shaftesbury">Earl of Shaftesbury</a>, Locke participated in drafting the <i><a href="/wiki/Fundamental_Constitutions_of_Carolina" title="Fundamental Constitutions of Carolina">Fundamental Constitutions of Carolina</a></i>, which established a <a href="/wiki/Feudalism" title="Feudalism">quasi-feudal</a> <a href="/wiki/Aristocracy" title="Aristocracy">aristocracy</a> and gave Carolinian <a href="/wiki/Planter_class" title="Planter class">planters</a> absolute power over their enslaved chattel property; the constitutions pledged that "every freeman of Carolina shall have absolute power and authority over his negro slaves". Philosopher <a href="/wiki/Martin_Cohen_(philosopher)" title="Martin Cohen (philosopher)">Martin Cohen</a> notes that Locke, as secretary to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Council_of_Trade_and_Plantations" title="Council of Trade and Plantations">Council of Trade and Plantations</a> and a member of the <a href="/wiki/Board_of_Trade" title="Board of Trade">Board of Trade</a>, was "one of just half a dozen men who created and supervised both the colonies and their iniquitous systems of servitude".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[43]</a></sup> 
According to American historian James Farr, Locke never expressed any thoughts concerning his contradictory opinions regarding slavery, which Farr ascribes to his personal involvement in the slave trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[44]</a></sup> Locke's positions on slavery have been described as hypocritical, and laying the foundation for the <a href="/wiki/Founding_Fathers_of_the_United_States" title="Founding Fathers of the United States">Founding Fathers</a> to hold similarly contradictory thoughts regarding freedom and slavery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[45]</a></sup> Locke also drafted implementing instructions for the Carolina colonists designed to ensure that settlement and development was consistent with the Fundamental Constitutions. Collectively, these documents are known as the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Model_for_the_Province_of_Carolina" title="Grand Model for the Province of Carolina">Grand Model for the Province of Carolina</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Historian Holly Brewer has argued, however, that Locke's role in the Constitution of Carolina has been exaggerated and that he was merely paid to revise and make copies of a document that had already been partially written before he became involved; she compares Locke's role to a lawyer writing a will.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrewer20171052_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrewer20171052-47">[46]</a></sup> She further notes that Locke was paid in Royal African Company stock in lieu of money for his work as a secretary for a governmental sub-committee and that he sold the stock after only a few years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrewer20171053–1054_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrewer20171053–1054-48">[47]</a></sup> Brewer likewise argues that Locke actively worked to undermine slavery in Virginia while heading a Board of Trade created by William of Orange following the Glorious Revolution. He specifically attacked colonial policy granting land to slave owners and encouraged the baptism and Christian education of the children of enslaved Africans to undercut a major justification of slavery—that they were heathens that possessed no rights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrewer20171066_&amp;_1072_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrewer20171066_&amp;_1072-49">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>Locke also supported <a href="/wiki/Child_labour" title="Child labour">child labour</a>. In his "Essay on the Poor Law", he turns to the education of the poor; he laments that "the children of labouring people are an ordinary burden to the parish, and are usually maintained in idleness, so that their labour also is generally lost to the public till they are 12 or 14 years old".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-poorlaw_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-poorlaw-50">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 190">: 190 </span></sup> He suggests, therefore, that "working schools" be set up in each parish in England for poor children so that they will be "from infancy [three years old] inured to work".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-poorlaw_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-poorlaw-50">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 190">: 190 </span></sup> He goes on to outline the economics of these schools, arguing not only that they will be profitable for the parish, but also that they will instill a good work ethic in the children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-poorlaw_50-2"><a href="#cite_note-poorlaw-50">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 191">: 191 </span></sup>
</p>
