<li><a href="/wiki/Progressivism" title="Progressivism">Progressivism</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sex-positive_feminism" title="Sex-positive feminism">Sexually liberal feminism</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Utilitarianism" title="Utilitarianism">Utilitarianism</a></li></ul></div></div></td>
</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-below plainlist">
<ul><li><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yellow_flag_waving.svg"><img alt="icon" class="noviewer" data-file-height="268" data-file-width="249" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Yellow_flag_waving.svg/15px-Yellow_flag_waving.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Yellow_flag_waving.svg/22px-Yellow_flag_waving.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Yellow_flag_waving.svg/30px-Yellow_flag_waving.svg.png 2x" width="15"/></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Liberalism" title="Portal:Liberalism">Liberalism portal</a></li>
<li><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_coloured_voting_box.svg"><img alt="icon" class="noviewer thumbborder" data-file-height="160" data-file-width="160" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/01/A_coloured_voting_box.svg/16px-A_coloured_voting_box.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/01/A_coloured_voting_box.svg/24px-A_coloured_voting_box.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/01/A_coloured_voting_box.svg/32px-A_coloured_voting_box.svg.png 2x" width="16"/></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Politics" title="Portal:Politics">Politics portal</a></li></ul></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1063604349" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Liberalism_sidebar" title="Template:Liberalism sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Liberalism_sidebar" title="Template talk:Liberalism sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Liberalism_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Locke's political theory was founded upon that of <a href="/wiki/Social_contract" title="Social contract">social contract</a>. Unlike <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hobbes" title="Thomas Hobbes">Thomas Hobbes</a>, Locke believed that <a href="/wiki/Human_nature" title="Human nature">human nature</a> is characterised by <a href="/wiki/Reason" title="Reason">reason</a> and <a href="/wiki/Toleration" title="Toleration">tolerance</a>. Like Hobbes, however, Locke believed that human nature allows people to be selfish. This is apparent with the introduction of currency. In a <a href="/wiki/State_of_nature#Locke's_view_on_the_state_of_nature" title="State of nature">natural state</a>, all people were equal and independent, and everyone had a natural right to defend his "life, health, liberty, or possessions".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-locke_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-locke-51">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 198">: 198 </span></sup> Most scholars trace the phrase "<a href="/wiki/Life,_Liberty_and_the_pursuit_of_Happiness" title="Life, Liberty and the pursuit of Happiness">Life, Liberty and the pursuit of Happiness</a>" in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence" title="United States Declaration of Independence">American Declaration of Independence</a> to Locke's theory of rights,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[51]</a></sup> although other origins have been suggested.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>Like Hobbes, Locke assumed that the sole right to defend in the state of nature was not enough, so people established a <a href="/wiki/Civil_society" title="Civil society">civil society</a> to resolve conflicts in a civil way with help from government in a state of society. However, Locke never refers to Hobbes by name and may instead have been responding to other writers of the day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[53]</a></sup> Locke also advocated governmental <a href="/wiki/Separation_of_powers" title="Separation of powers">separation of powers</a> and believed that revolution is not only a <a href="/wiki/Right_of_revolution" title="Right of revolution">right</a> but an obligation in some circumstances. These ideas would come to have profound influence on the <a href="/wiki/Declaration_of_independence" title="Declaration of independence">Declaration of Independence</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_the_United_States" title="Constitution of the United States">Constitution of the United States</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Accumulation_of_wealth">Accumulation of wealth</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Lockean_proviso" title="Lockean proviso">Lockean proviso</a></div>
<p>According to Locke, unused property is wasteful and an offence against nature,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[54]</a></sup> but, with the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Durable_good" title="Durable good">"durable" goods</a>, men could exchange their excessive perishable goods for those which would last longer and thus not offend the <a href="/wiki/Natural_law" title="Natural law">natural law</a>. In his view, the introduction of money marked the culmination of this process, making possible the unlimited accumulation of property without causing waste through spoilage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[55]</a></sup> He also includes gold or silver as money because they may be "hoarded up without injury to anyone",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[56]</a></sup> as they do not spoil or decay in the hands of the possessor. In his view, the introduction of money eliminates limits to accumulation. Locke stresses that inequality has come about by tacit agreement on the use of money, not by the social contract establishing <a href="/wiki/Civil_society" title="Civil society">civil society</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Land_law" title="Land law">law of land</a> regulating property. Locke is aware of a problem posed by unlimited accumulation, but does not consider it his task. He just implies that government would function to moderate the conflict between the unlimited accumulation of property and a more nearly equal distribution of wealth; he does not identify which principles that government should apply to solve this problem. However, not all elements of his thought form a consistent whole. For example, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Labour_theory_of_value" title="Labour theory of value">labour theory of value</a> in the <i><a href="/wiki/Two_Treatises_of_Government" title="Two Treatises of Government">Two Treatises of Government</a></i> stands side by side with the demand-and-supply theory of value developed in a letter he wrote titled <i>Some Considerations on the Consequences of the Lowering of Interest and the Raising of the Value of Money</i>. Moreover, Locke anchors property in labour but, in the end, upholds unlimited accumulation of wealth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[57]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ideas">Ideas</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economics">Economics</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="On_price_theory">On price theory</span></h4>
<p>Locke's general theory of value and price is a <a href="/wiki/Supply_and_demand" title="Supply and demand">supply-and-demand</a> theory, set out in a letter to a member of parliament in 1691, titled <i>Some Considerations on the Consequences of the Lowering of Interest and the Raising of the Value of Money</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[58]</a></sup> In it, he refers to supply as <i>quantity</i> and demand as <a href="/wiki/Economic_rent" title="Economic rent"><i>rent</i></a>: "The price of any commodity rises or falls by the proportion of the number of buyers and sellers" and "that which regulates the price…[of goods] is nothing else but their quantity in proportion to their rent."
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Quantity_theory_of_money" title="Quantity theory of money">quantity theory of money</a> forms a special case of this general theory. His idea is based on "money answers all things" (<a href="/wiki/Ecclesiastes" title="Ecclesiastes">Ecclesiastes</a>) or "rent of money is always sufficient, or more than enough" and "varies very little". Locke concludes that, as far as money is concerned, the <a href="/wiki/Demand_for_money" title="Demand for money">demand</a> for it is exclusively regulated by its quantity, regardless of whether the demand is unlimited or constant. He also investigates the determinants of demand and supply. For <a href="/wiki/Supply_(economics)" title="Supply (economics)">supply</a>, he explains the value of goods as based on their <a href="/wiki/Scarcity" title="Scarcity">scarcity</a> and ability to be <a href="/wiki/Exchange_value" title="Exchange value">exchanged</a> and <a href="/wiki/Consumption_(economics)" title="Consumption (economics)">consumed</a>. He explains <a href="/wiki/Demand" title="Demand">demand</a> for goods as based on their ability to yield a flow of income. Locke develops an early theory of <a href="/wiki/Capital_(economics)" title="Capital (economics)">capitalisation</a>, such as of land, which has value because "by its constant production of saleable <a href="/wiki/Commodity" title="Commodity">commodities</a> it brings in a certain yearly income". He considers the demand for money as almost the same as demand for goods or land: it depends on whether money is wanted as <a href="/wiki/Medium_of_exchange" title="Medium of exchange">medium of exchange</a>. As a medium of exchange, he states, "money is capable by exchange to procure us the necessaries or conveniences of life" and, for <a href="/wiki/Loanable_funds" title="Loanable funds">loanable funds</a>, "it comes to be of the same nature with land by yielding a certain yearly income…or interest".
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Monetary_thoughts">Monetary thoughts</span></h4>
<p>Locke distinguishes two functions of money: as a <i>counter</i> to <a href="/wiki/Valuation_(finance)" title="Valuation (finance)">measure value</a>, and as a <i>pledge</i> to lay claim to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Good_(economics)" title="Good (economics)">goods</a>. He believes that silver and gold, as opposed to <a href="/wiki/Banknote" title="Banknote">paper money</a>, are the appropriate currency for international transactions. Silver and gold, he says, are treated to have equal value by all of humanity and can thus be treated as a pledge by anyone, while the value of paper money is only valid under the government which issues it.
</p><p>Locke argues that a country should seek a favourable <a href="/wiki/Balance_of_trade" title="Balance of trade">balance of trade</a>, lest it fall behind other countries and suffer a loss in its trade. Since the world <a href="/wiki/Money_supply" title="Money supply">money stock</a> grows constantly, a country must constantly seek to enlarge its own stock. Locke develops his theory of foreign exchanges, in addition to commodity movements, there are also movements in country stock of money, and movements of capital determine <a href="/wiki/Exchange_rate" title="Exchange rate">exchange rates</a>. He considers the latter less significant and less <a href="/wiki/Volatility_(finance)" title="Volatility (finance)">volatile</a> than commodity movements. As for a country's money stock, if it is large relative to that of other countries, he says it will cause the country's exchange to rise above par, as an export balance would do.
</p><p>He also prepares estimates of the <a href="/wiki/Cash" title="Cash">cash</a> requirements for different economic groups (<a href="/wiki/Land_tenure" title="Land tenure">landholders</a>, labourers, and brokers). In each group he posits that the cash requirements are closely related to the length of the pay period. He argues the brokers—the <a href="/wiki/Intermediary" title="Intermediary">middlemen</a>—whose activities enlarge the monetary circuit and whose profits eat into the earnings of labourers and landholders, have a negative influence on both personal and the public economy to which they supposedly contribute.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Theory_of_value_and_property">Theory of value and property</span></h4>
<p>Locke uses the concept of <i><a href="/wiki/Property_(philosophy)" title="Property (philosophy)">property</a></i> in both broad and narrow terms: broadly, it covers a wide range of human interests and aspirations; more particularly, it refers to <a href="/wiki/Tangible_property" title="Tangible property">material goods</a>. He argues that property is a <a href="/wiki/Natural_rights_and_legal_rights" title="Natural rights and legal rights">natural right</a> that is derived from <a href="/wiki/Manual_labour" title="Manual labour">labour</a>. In Chapter V of his <i><a href="/wiki/Two_Treatises_of_Government" title="Two Treatises of Government">Second Treatise</a></i>, Locke argues that the individual ownership of goods and property is justified by the labour exerted to produce such goods—"at least where there is enough [land], and as good, left in common for others" (para. 27)—or to use property to produce goods beneficial to human society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-labortheory_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-labortheory-60">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>Locke states in his <i>Second Treatise</i> that nature on its own provides little of value to society, implying that the labour expended in the creation of goods gives them their value. From this premise, understood as a <a href="/wiki/Labor_theory_of_value" title="Labor theory of value"><i>labour theory of value</i></a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-labortheory_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-labortheory-60">[59]</a></sup> Locke developed a <a href="/wiki/Labor_theory_of_property" title="Labor theory of property"><i>labour theory of property</i></a>, whereby ownership of <a href="/wiki/Property" title="Property">property</a> is created by the application of labour. In addition, he believed that property precedes government and government cannot "dispose of the estates of the subjects arbitrarily". <a href="/wiki/Karl_Marx" title="Karl Marx">Karl Marx</a> later critiqued Locke's theory of property in his own social theory.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_human_mind">The human mind</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_self">The self</span></h4>
<p>Locke defines <i>the self</i> as "that conscious thinking thing, (whatever substance, made up of whether spiritual, or material, simple, or compounded, it matters not) which is sensible, or conscious of pleasure and pain, capable of happiness or misery, and so is concerned for itself, as far as that consciousness extends".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELocke1997b307_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELocke1997b307-61">[60]</a></sup> He does not, however, wholly ignore "substance", writing that "the body too goes to the making the man".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELocke1997b306_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELocke1997b306-62">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>In his <i>Essay</i>, Locke explains the gradual unfolding of this conscious mind. Arguing against both the <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">Augustinian</a> view of man as <a href="/wiki/Original_sin" title="Original sin">originally sinful</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="René Descartes">Cartesian</a> position, which holds that man innately knows basic logical propositions, Locke posits an 'empty mind', a <i><a href="/wiki/Tabula_rasa" title="Tabula rasa">tabula rasa</a></i>, which is shaped by experience; <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/sensation" title="wikt:sensation">sensations</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_self-reflection" title="Human self-reflection">reflections</a> being the two sources of <i>all</i> of our <a href="/wiki/Idea" title="Idea">ideas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[62]</a></sup> He states in <i>An Essay Concerning Human Understanding</i>:
</p>
<blockquote><p>This source of ideas every man has wholly within himself; and though it be not sense, as having nothing to do with external objects, yet it is very like it, and might properly enough be called 'internal sense.'<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[63]</a></sup></p></blockquote><p>
Locke's <i><a href="/wiki/Some_Thoughts_Concerning_Education" title="Some Thoughts Concerning Education">Some Thoughts Concerning Education</a></i> is an outline on how to educate this mind. Drawing on thoughts expressed in letters written to <a href="/wiki/Mary_Clarke_(letter_writer)" title="Mary Clarke (letter writer)">Mary Clarke</a> and her husband about their son,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MaryC_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-MaryC-65">[64]</a></sup> he expresses the belief that education makes the man—or, more fundamentally, that the mind is an "empty cabinet":<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELocke199610_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELocke199610-66">[65]</a></sup></p><blockquote><p>I think I may say that of all the men we meet with, nine parts of ten are what they are, good or evil, useful or not, by their education.</p></blockquote><p>Locke also wrote that "the little and almost insensible impressions on our tender infancies have very important and lasting consequences".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELocke199610_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELocke199610-66">[65]</a></sup> He argues that the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Association_of_Ideas" title="Association of Ideas">associations of ideas</a>" that one makes when young are more important than those made later because they are the foundation of the <i>self</i>; they are, put differently, what first mark the <i>tabula rasa</i>. In his <i>Essay</i>, in which both these concepts are introduced, Locke warns, for example, against letting "a foolish maid" convince a child that "goblins and sprites" are associated with the night, for "darkness shall ever afterwards bring with it those frightful ideas, and they shall be so joined, that he can no more bear the one than the other".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELocke1997b357_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELocke1997b357-67">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>This theory came to be called <i><a href="/wiki/Associationism" title="Associationism">associationism</a></i>, going on to strongly influence 18th-century thought, particularly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Education_theory" title="Education theory">educational theory</a>, as nearly every educational writer warned parents not to allow their children to develop negative associations. It also led to the development of <a href="/wiki/Psychology" title="Psychology">psychology</a> and other new disciplines with <a href="/wiki/David_Hartley_(philosopher)" title="David Hartley (philosopher)">David Hartley</a>'s attempt to discover a biological mechanism for associationism in his <i><a href="/wiki/Observations_on_Man" title="Observations on Man">Observations on Man</a></i> (1749).
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Dream_argument">Dream argument</span></h4>
<p>Locke was critical of Descartes's version of the <a href="/wiki/Dream_argument" title="Dream argument">dream argument</a>, with Locke making the counter-argument that people cannot have physical pain in dreams as they do in waking life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_beliefs">Religious beliefs</span></h4>
<p>Some scholars have seen Locke's political convictions as being based from his religious beliefs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[70]</a></sup> Locke's religious trajectory began in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calvinist" title="Calvinist">Calvinist</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trinitarianism" title="Trinitarianism">trinitarianism</a>, but by the time of the <i>Reflections</i> (1695) Locke was advocating not just <a href="/wiki/Socinianism" title="Socinianism">Socinian views</a> on tolerance but also Socinian <a href="/wiki/Christology" title="Christology">Christology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marshall_1994_426_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marshall_1994_426-72">[71]</a></sup> However Wainwright (1987) notes that in the posthumously published <i>Paraphrase</i> (1707) Locke's interpretation of one verse, <a href="/wiki/Ephesians_1" title="Ephesians 1">Ephesians 1</a>:10, is markedly different from that of Socinians like <a href="/wiki/John_Biddle_(Unitarian)" title="John Biddle (Unitarian)">Biddle</a>, and may indicate that near the end of his life Locke returned nearer to an <a href="/wiki/Arianism" title="Arianism">Arian</a> position, thereby accepting Christ's pre-existence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marshall_1994_426_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-Marshall_1994_426-72">[71]</a></sup>
Locke was at times not sure about the subject of <a href="/wiki/Original_sin" title="Original sin">original sin</a>, so he was accused of Socinianism, Arianism, or <a href="/wiki/Deism" title="Deism">Deism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldron200227,_223_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldron200227,_223-74">[73]</a></sup> Locke argued that the idea that "all <i><a href="/wiki/Adam" title="Adam">Adam</a>'</i>s Posterity [are] doomed to Eternal Infinite Punishment, for the Transgression of <i>Adam</i>" was "little consistent with the Justice or Goodness of the Great and Infinite God", leading <a href="/wiki/Eric_Nelson_(historian)" title="Eric Nelson (historian)">Eric Nelson</a> to associate him with <a href="/wiki/Pelagianism" title="Pelagianism">Pelagian</a> ideas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson20197–8_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson20197–8-75">[74]</a></sup> However, he did not deny the reality of evil. Man was capable of waging unjust wars and committing crimes. Criminals had to be punished, even with the death penalty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldron2002145_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldron2002145-76">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>With regard to the Bible, Locke was very conservative. He retained the doctrine of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Verbal_inspiration" title="Verbal inspiration">verbal inspiration</a> of the Scriptures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeussi1956_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeussi1956-37">[36]</a></sup> The miracles were proof of the divine nature of the biblical message. Locke was convinced that the entire content of the Bible was in agreement with human reason (<i>The Reasonableness of Christianity</i>, 1695).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeussi1956_37-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeussi1956-37">[36]</a></sup> Although Locke was an advocate of tolerance, he urged the authorities not to tolerate <a href="/wiki/Atheism" title="Atheism">atheism</a>, because he thought the denial of God's existence would undermine the social order and lead to chaos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldron2002217_ff_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldron2002217_ff-78">[77]</a></sup> That excluded all atheistic varieties of philosophy and all attempts to deduce ethics and natural law from purely secular premises.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldron200213_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldron200213-79">[78]</a></sup> In Locke's opinion the <a href="/wiki/Cosmological_argument" title="Cosmological argument">cosmological argument</a> was valid and proved God's existence. His political thought was based on Protestant Christian views.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldron200213_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldron200213-79">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[79]</a></sup> Additionally, Locke advocated a sense of piety out of gratitude to God for giving reason to men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[80]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Philosophy_from_religion">Philosophy from religion</span></h4>
<p>Locke's concept of man started with the belief in creation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldron2002142_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldron2002142-82">[81]</a></sup> Like philosophers <a href="/wiki/Hugo_Grotius" title="Hugo Grotius">Hugo Grotius</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Samuel_Pufendorf" title="Samuel Pufendorf">Samuel Pufendorf</a>, Locke equated <a href="/wiki/Natural_law" title="Natural law">natural law</a> with the biblical <a href="/wiki/Revelation" title="Revelation">revelation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldron200212_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldron200212-85">[84]</a></sup> Locke derived the fundamental concepts of his political theory from biblical texts, in particular from <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Genesis" title="Book of Genesis">Genesis</a> 1 and 2 (<a href="/wiki/Genesis_creation_narrative" title="Genesis creation narrative">creation</a>), the <a href="/wiki/Ten_Commandments" title="Ten Commandments">Decalogue</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Rule" title="Golden Rule">Golden Rule</a>, the teachings of Jesus, and the letters of <a href="/wiki/Paul_the_Apostle" title="Paul the Apostle">Paul the Apostle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldron200222–43,_45–46,_101,_153–58,_195,_197_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldron200222–43,_45–46,_101,_153–58,_195,_197-86">[85]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ten_Commandments" title="Ten Commandments">The Decalogue</a> puts a person's life, reputation and property under God's protection.
</p><p>Locke's philosophy on freedom is also derived from the Bible. Locke derived from the Bible basic human equality (including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Equality_of_the_sexes" title="Equality of the sexes">equality of the sexes</a>), the starting point of the theological doctrine of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imago_Dei" title="Imago Dei">Imago Dei</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldron200221–43_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldron200221–43-87">[86]</a></sup> To Locke, one of the consequences of the principle of equality was that all humans were created equally free and therefore governments needed the consent of the governed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldron2002136_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldron2002136-88">[87]</a></sup> Locke compared the English monarchy's rule over the British people to Adam's rule over Eve in Genesis, which was appointed by God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>Following Locke's philosophy, the American <a href="/wiki/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence" title="United States Declaration of Independence">Declaration of Independence</a> founded human rights partially on the biblical belief in creation. Locke's doctrine that governments need the consent of the governed is also central to the Declaration of Independence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[89]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Library">Library</span></h2>
<h3><span id="Manuscripts.2C_books_and_treatises"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Manuscripts,_books_and_treatises">Manuscripts, books and treatises</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Locke_Signature.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="365" data-file-width="625" decoding="async" height="128" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Locke_Signature.jpg/220px-Locke_Signature.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Locke_Signature.jpg/330px-Locke_Signature.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Locke_Signature.jpg/440px-Locke_Signature.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Locke_Signature.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Locke's signature in Bodleian Locke 13.12. Photo taken at the Bodleian Library, Oxford.</div></div></div>
<p>Locke was an assiduous book collector and notetaker throughout his life. By his death in 1704, Locke had amassed a library of more than 3,000 books, a significant number in the seventeenth century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[90]</a></sup> Unlike some of his contemporaries, Locke took care to catalogue and preserve his library, and his will made specific provisions for how his library was to be distributed after his death. Locke's will offered Lady Masham the choice of "any four folios, eight quartos and twenty books of less volume, which she shall choose out of the books in my Library."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-92">[91]</a></sup> Locke also gave six titles to his “good friend” <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Collins_(philosopher)" title="Anthony Collins (philosopher)">Anthony Collins</a>, but Locke bequeathed the majority of his collection to his cousin <a href="/wiki/Peter_King,_1st_Baron_King" title="Peter King, 1st Baron King">Peter King</a> (later Lord King) and to Lady Masham's son, Francis Cudworth Masham.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-92">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p>Francis Masham was promised one “moiety” (half) of Locke's library when he reached “the age of one and twenty years.”<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_92-2"><a href="#cite_note-:1-92">[91]</a></sup> The other “moiety” of Locke's books, along with his manuscripts, passed to his cousin King.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_92-3"><a href="#cite_note-:1-92">[91]</a></sup> Over the next two centuries, the Masham portion of Locke's library was dispersed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[92]</a></sup> The manuscripts and books left to King, however, remained with King's descendants (later the <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Lovelace" title="Earl of Lovelace">Earls of Lovelace</a>), until most of the collection was bought by the <a href="/wiki/Bodleian_Library" title="Bodleian Library">Bodleian Library, Oxford</a> in 1947.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-94">[93]</a></sup> Another portion of the books Locke left to King was discovered by the collector and philanthropist <a href="/wiki/Paul_Mellon" title="Paul Mellon">Paul Mellon</a> in 1951.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-94">[93]</a></sup> Mellon supplemented this discovery with books from Locke's library which he bought privately, and in 1978, he transferred his collection to the Bodleian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_94-2"><a href="#cite_note-:2-94">[93]</a></sup> The holdings in the Locke Room at the Bodleian have been a valuable resource for scholars interested in Locke, his philosophy, practices for information management, and the history of the book.
</p><p>Many of the books still contain Locke's signature, which he often made on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pastedown" title="Pastedown">pastedowns</a> of his books. Many also include Locke's <a href="/wiki/Marginalia" title="Marginalia">marginalia</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Locke_treatises_of_government_page.jpg"><img alt="image of locke's treatises of government" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="981" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="337" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Locke_treatises_of_government_page.jpg/220px-Locke_treatises_of_government_page.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Locke_treatises_of_government_page.jpg/330px-Locke_treatises_of_government_page.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Locke_treatises_of_government_page.jpg/440px-Locke_treatises_of_government_page.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Locke_treatises_of_government_page.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>One of Locke's famous books on politics, <a href="/wiki/Two_Treatises_of_Government" title="Two Treatises of Government">Two Treatises of Government</a>, written and published in his lifetime.</div></div></div>
<p>The printed books in Locke's library reflected his various intellectual interests as well as his movements at different stages of his life. Locke travelled extensively in France and the Netherlands during the 1670s and 1680s, and during this time he acquired many books from the continent. Only half of the books in Locke's library were printed in England, while close to 40% came from France and the Netherlands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[94]</a></sup> These books cover a wide range of subjects. According to John Harrison and Peter Laslett, the largest genres in Locke's library were <a href="/wiki/Theology" title="Theology">theology</a> (23.8% of books), medicine (11.1%), politics and law (10.7%), and classical literature (10.1%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[95]</a></sup> The Bodleian library currently holds more than 800 of the books from Locke's library.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_94-3"><a href="#cite_note-:2-94">[93]</a></sup> These include Locke's copies of works by several of the most influential figures of the seventeenth century, including
</p>
<ul><li>The Quaker <a href="/wiki/William_Penn" title="William Penn">William Penn</a>: <i>An address to Protestants of all perswasions</i> (Bodleian Locke 7.69a)</li>
<li>The explorer <a href="/wiki/Francis_Drake" title="Francis Drake">Francis Drake</a>: <i>The world encompassed by Sir Francis Drake</i> (Bodleian Locke 8.37c)</li>
<li>The scientist <a href="/wiki/Robert_Boyle" title="Robert Boyle">Robert Boyle</a>: <i>A discourse of things above reason</i> (Bodleian Locke 7.272)</li>
<li>The bishop and historian <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Sprat" title="Thomas Sprat">Thomas Sprat</a>: <i>The history of the Royal-Society of London</i> (Bodleian Locke 9.10a)</li></ul>
<p>In addition to books owned by Locke, the Bodleian also possesses more than 100 <a href="/wiki/Manuscript" title="Manuscript">manuscripts</a> related to Locke or written in his hand. Like the books in Locke's library, these manuscripts display a range of interests and provide different windows into Locke's activity and relationships. Several of the manuscripts include letters to and from acquaintances like Peter King (MS Locke b. 6) and <a class="extiw" href="https://fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nicolas_Toinard" title="fr:Nicolas Toinard">Nicolas Toinard</a> (MS Locke c. 45).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-97">[96]</a></sup> MS Locke f. 1–10 contain Locke's journals for most years between 1675 and 1704.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-97">[96]</a></sup> Some of the most significant manuscripts include early drafts of Locke's writings, such as his <i><a href="/wiki/An_Essay_Concerning_Human_Understanding" title="An Essay Concerning Human Understanding">Essay concerning human understanding</a></i> (MS Locke f. 26).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_97-2"><a href="#cite_note-:3-97">[96]</a></sup> The Bodleian also holds a copy of Robert Boyle's <i>General History of the Air</i> with corrections and notes Locke made while preparing Boyle's work for posthumous publication (MS Locke c. 37 ).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[97]</a></sup> Other manuscripts contain unpublished works. Among others, MS. Locke e. 18 includes some of Locke's thoughts on the <a href="/wiki/Glorious_Revolution" title="Glorious Revolution">Glorious Revolution</a>, which Locke sent to his friend Edward Clarke but never published.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>One of the largest categories of manuscript at the Bodleian comprises Locke's notebooks and <a href="/wiki/Commonplace_book" title="Commonplace book">commonplace books</a>. The scholar Richard Yeo calls Locke a "Master Note-taker" and explains that "Locke's methodical note-taking pervaded most areas of his life."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[99]</a></sup> In an unpublished essay “Of Study,” Locke argued that a notebook should work like a “chest-of-drawers” for organizing information, which would be a "great help to the memory and means to avoid confusion in our thoughts."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[100]</a></sup> Locke kept several notebooks and commonplace books, which he organized according to topic. MS Locke c. 43 includes Locke's notes on theology, while MS Locke f. 18–24 contain medical notes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_97-3"><a href="#cite_note-:3-97">[96]</a></sup> Other notebooks, such as MS c. 43, incorporate several topics in the same notebook, but separated into sections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_97-4"><a href="#cite_note-:3-97">[96]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Locke_Index,_page_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2019" data-file-width="1481" decoding="async" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Locke_Index%2C_page_1.jpg/220px-Locke_Index%2C_page_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Locke_Index%2C_page_1.jpg/330px-Locke_Index%2C_page_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Locke_Index%2C_page_1.jpg/440px-Locke_Index%2C_page_1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Locke_Index,_page_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Page 1 of Locke's unfinished index in Bodleian Locke 13.12. Photo taken at the Bodleian Library, Oxford.</div></div></div>
<p>These commonplace books were highly personal and were designed to be used by Locke himself rather than accessible to a wide audience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[101]</a></sup> Locke's notes are often abbreviated and are full of codes which he used to reference material across notebooks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[102]</a></sup> Another way Locke personalized his notebooks was by devising his own method of creating indexes using a grid system and Latin keywords.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[103]</a></sup> Instead of recording entire words, his indexes shortened words to their first letter and vowel. Thus, the word "Epistle" would be classified as "Ei".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[104]</a></sup> Locke published his method in French in 1686, and it was <a class="extiw" href="//archive.org/details/gu_newmethodmaki00lock" title="iarchive:gu newmethodmaki00lock">republished posthumously in English</a> in 1706.
</p><p>Some of the books in Locke's library at the Bodleian are a combination of manuscript and print. Locke had some of his books interleaved, meaning that they were bound with blank sheets in-between the printed pages to enable annotations. Locke interleaved and annotated his five volumes of the New Testament in French, Greek, and Latin (Bodleian Locke 9.103-107). Locke did the same with his copy of Thomas Hyde's Bodleian Library catalogue (Bodleian Locke 16.17), which Locke used to create a catalogue of his own library.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[105]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Writing">Writing</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="List_of_major_works">List of major works</span></h3>
<ul><li>1689. <i><a href="/wiki/A_Letter_Concerning_Toleration" title="A Letter Concerning Toleration">A Letter Concerning Toleration</a></i>.
<ul><li>1690. <i>A Second Letter Concerning Toleration</i></li>
<li>1692. <i>A Third Letter for Toleration</i></li></ul></li>
<li>1689/90. <i><a href="/wiki/Two_Treatises_of_Government" title="Two Treatises of Government">Two Treatises of Government</a></i> (published throughout the 18th century by London bookseller <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Millar" title="Andrew Millar">Andrew Millar</a> by commission for <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hollis_(1720%E2%80%931774)" title="Thomas Hollis (1720–1774)">Thomas Hollis</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[106]</a></sup></li>
<li>1689/90. <i><a href="/wiki/An_Essay_Concerning_Human_Understanding" title="An Essay Concerning Human Understanding">An Essay Concerning Human Understanding</a></i></li>
<li>1691. <i>Some Considerations on the consequences of the Lowering of Interest and the Raising of the Value of Money</i></li>
<li>1693. <i><a href="/wiki/Some_Thoughts_Concerning_Education" title="Some Thoughts Concerning Education">Some Thoughts Concerning Education</a></i></li>
<li>1695. <i>The Reasonableness of Christianity, as Delivered in the Scriptures</i>
<ul><li>1695. <i>A Vindication of the Reasonableness of Christianity</i></li></ul></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Major_posthumous_manuscripts">Major posthumous manuscripts</span></h3>
<ul><li>1660. <i>First Tract of Government</i> (or <i>the English Tract</i>)</li>
<li><i>c.</i>1662. <i>Second Tract of Government</i> (or <i>the Latin Tract</i>)</li>
<li>1664. <i>Questions Concerning the Law of Nature</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[107]</a></sup></li>
<li>1667. <i>Essay Concerning Toleration</i></li>
<li>1706. <i><a href="/wiki/Of_the_Conduct_of_the_Understanding" title="Of the Conduct of the Understanding">Of the Conduct of the Understanding</a></i></li>
<li>1707. <i>A paraphrase and notes on the Epistles of St. Paul to the Galatians, 1 and 2 Corinthians, Romans, Ephesians</i></li></ul>

Wiley-Blackwell. p. 43. <q>All of Descartes, Locke, Berkeley, and Hume supposed that mathematics is a theory of our <i>ideas</i>, but none of them offered any argument for this conceptualist claim, and apparently took it to be uncontroversial.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Philosophy+of+Mathematics%3A+An+Introduction&amp;rft.pages=43&amp;rft.pub=Wiley-Blackwell&amp;rft.date=2009&amp;rft.au=David+Bostock&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AJohn+Locke"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-30"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-30">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1"><a class="external text" href="https://www.loc.gov/exhibits/treasures/tr11a.html#obj11" rel="nofollow">"The Three Greatest Men"</a>. <i>American Treasures of the Library of Congress</i>. <ul><li><a href="/wiki/Richard_Ashcraft" title="Richard Ashcraft">Ashcraft, Richard</a>, 1986.  <i>Revolutionary Politics &amp; Locke's Two Treatises of Government.</i>  Princeton: Princeton University Press. Discusses the relationship between Locke's philosophy and his political activities.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Michael_R._Ayers" title="Michael R. Ayers">Ayers, Michael</a>, 1991. <i>Locke. Epistemology &amp; Ontology</i> Routledge (the standard work on Locke's Essay Concerning Human Understanding.)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bernard_Bailyn" title="Bernard Bailyn">Bailyn, Bernard</a>, 1992 (1967). <i><a href="/wiki/The_Ideological_Origins_of_the_American_Revolution" title="The Ideological Origins of the American Revolution">The Ideological Origins of the American Revolution</a></i>. Harvard Uni. Press. Discusses the influence of Locke and other thinkers upon the American Revolution and on subsequent American political thought.</li>
<li><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFBrewer2017">Brewer, Holly (October 2017). <a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1093/ahr/122.4.1038" rel="nofollow">"Slavery, Sovereignty, and "Inheritable Blood": Reconsidering John Locke and the Origins of American Slavery"</a>. <i>American Historical Review</i>. <b>122</b> (4): 1038–1078. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fahr%2F122.4.1038" rel="nofollow">10.1093/ahr/122.4.1038</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=American+Historical+Review&amp;rft.atitle=Slavery%2C+Sovereignty%2C+and+%22Inheritable+Blood%22%3A+Reconsidering+John+Locke+and+the+Origins+of+American+Slavery&amp;rft.volume=122&amp;rft.issue=4&amp;rft.pages=1038-1078&amp;rft.date=2017-10&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1093%2Fahr%2F122.4.1038&amp;rft.aulast=Brewer&amp;rft.aufirst=Holly&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fdoi.org%2F10.1093%2Fahr%2F122.4.1038&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AJohn+Locke"></span></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gerald_Cohen" title="Gerald Cohen">Cohen, Gerald</a>, 1995. 'Marx and Locke on Land and Labour', in his <i>Self-Ownership, Freedom and Equality</i>, Oxford University Press.</li>
<li>Cox, Richard, <i>Locke on War and Peace</i>, Oxford: Oxford University Press, 1960. A discussion of Locke's theory of international relations.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Vere_Claiborne_Chappell" title="Vere Claiborne Chappell">Chappell, Vere</a>, ed., 1994. <i>The Cambridge Companion to Locke</i>. Cambridge U.P. <a class="external text" href="https://www.amazon.com/Cambridge-Companion-Locke-Companions-Philosophy/dp/0521387728/" rel="nofollow">excerpt and text search</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/John_Dunn_(political_theorist)" title="John Dunn (political theorist)">Dunn, John</a>, 1984. <i>Locke</i>. Oxford Uni. Press. A succinct introduction.</li>
<li>———, 1969. <i>The Political Thought of John Locke: An Historical Account of the Argument of the "Two Treatises of Government"</i>. Cambridge Uni. Press. Introduced the interpretation which emphasises the theological element in Locke's political thought.</li>
<li><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation cs2 cs1-prop-foreign-lang-source" id="CITEREFHeussi1956">Heussi, Karl (1956), <i>Kompendium der Kirchengeschichte</i> (in German), Tübingen, DE</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Kompendium+der+Kirchengeschichte&amp;rft.place=T%C3%BCbingen%2C+DE&amp;rft.date=1956&amp;rft.aulast=Heussi&amp;rft.aufirst=Karl&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AJohn+Locke"></span></li>

"John Locke: from absolutism to toleration". <i><a href="/wiki/American_Political_Science_Review" title="American Political Science Review">American Political Science Review</a></i>. <b>74</b> (1): 53–69. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.2307%2F1955646" rel="nofollow">10.2307/1955646</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/JSTOR_(identifier)" title="JSTOR (identifier)">JSTOR</a> <a class="external text" href="//www.jstor.org/stable/1955646" rel="nofollow">1955646</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/S2CID_(identifier)" title="S2CID (identifier)">S2CID</a> <a class="external text" href="https://api.semanticscholar.org/CorpusID:146901427" rel="nofollow">146901427</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=American+Political+Science+Review&amp;rft.atitle=John+Locke%3A+from+absolutism+to+toleration&amp;rft.volume=74&amp;rft.issue=1&amp;rft.pages=53-69&amp;rft.date=1980-03&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fapi.semanticscholar.org%2FCorpusID%3A146901427%23id-name%3DS2CID&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.jstor.org%2Fstable%2F1955646%23id-name%3DJSTOR&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.2307%2F1955646&amp;rft.aulast=Kraynak&amp;rft.aufirst=Robert+P.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AJohn+Locke"></span></li>
